A tip...  I've made two countertops using this technique.  In both cases, there is a discolored blotch (lighter or darker) the size of a pancake on the surface at the spot where I poured the mortar into the mold.  So when you pour in the mortar, I recommend that you do it slowly and move the pour randomly around the mold.

I watched This Old House filmed locally many years ago as Norm and the current host praised the use and installation of concrete countertops. They had a hard time getting through their speil without laughing at what they were advocating. I still wonder what incentive got them to spout such nonsense. Yesrs later a client had such a kitchen countertop and was really needing information on what they could do to undo the continuing failures of that top on ordinary use. I studied that 4" slab of grey driveway material and told them that there was no catalytic resin that would help and no surface treatment that could remove all of the stains and scars except to grind down 1/4" to expose undamaged surface, but that would only be the start of another cycle of dissatisfaction. Their only solution was to jackhammer the slab into movable sections which would damage the cabinets requiring their replacement. Massive design fraud. The only attractive use of concrete is to embed attractive small rocks into the wet surface and when the slab has cured to grind the surface to expose the new terrazzo finish which then would need sealing. Great Video! You‚Äôre a natural in front of a camera. I just made an 8 ' bar counter top and it turned out great. I did add set control because it is 90 degrees outside. I also added 9 ga Ladder Mesh for reinforcement and a color additive. A couple of recommendations. Try to pour the concrete in a corner or on a side wall and never in the middle of another pour. That will make the color blend more natural. I had four 5-gallon buckets premixed with water, set control, and flow control before I began mixing the concrete. You will need a second hand to help knock this out quickly. But I completed this over a couple of days, including sanding and sealing. You could never do this with regular concrete.

Nice job, but you are ignoring the underlying first principles.  Concrete has high compressive strength, but relatively little tensile strength.  Any concrete slab laying horizontally will have an overhead dead load from its own weight and any other permanent load, and likely live loads coming down from above (snow load, people walking around, a car driving over it, or somebody sitting down on a concrete table).  When the load bears down or strikes down, the top of the slab goes into compression, and the bottom goes into tension, which is one of the main reasons concrete cracks.  The bottom goes into tension, and, lacking tensile strength, pulls apart, forming a crack.  The compression above the neutral axis (the horizontal center) is not the problem.  So whether your concrete has 5000psi compressive strength or 5000MPa makes little different.  The rebar goes in below the neutral axis (if the masons know what they are doing) to provide tensile strength in the lower portion, so it doesn't pull apart at the bottom, forming a crack.  With a coffee table, the loads are not so great, but the concrete is pretty thin.  Reinforcing bar and/or wire are advisable.  Anyway, nice looking project.  Don't dance on it.

Maybe I misinterpreted, but this concrete sets FAST and I made the mistake of pouring one bucket then mixing the other one after. Took a good ten minutes to mix so definitely mix all together and pour at the same time! Mine came out uneven because of this. Also, pay attention to the height of the sides of your mold. My concrete didn‚Äôt fill to the top so it was tough to scrape off easily. Besides these two frustrations everything in this video worked great!
Lessons Learned:  Place the mold on a flat surface.  I had it on sawhorses and it bent the mold. The bucket of mixed mortar is 65 pounds.  Be ready for that, especially if you are lifting it up high to pour. Make sure to place the stencil upside down so it comes out right side up. Doh! Use a bag of Set Control per batch in addition to the Flow Control.  It gives you about 20 minutes of delayed set time that you will need. The sanded grout doesn't move around, believe it or not. 5 quarts of water made the heavier aggregate separate to the bottom of the bucket.  Several people seemed to have success with 4 quarts. Good Luck!

You reason for not using steel reinforcement is invalid.  The rapid set has a high psi rating, that is in reference to its compression strength. Its tensile strength is anywhere from 300 to 600 psi. So it doesnt matter if your concrete is rated at 100,000 psi, if its tensile is at 300, it will crack under stress.

When you used the colored tile grout was it sanded or non sanded type?
I completed my first set of countertops and bar tops this week. Seven pieces, totaling approximately 35 square feet. This video helped me through the process, for sure. There are some things that I would like to add, in the hope of helping others‚Ä¶  If you are pouring countertops, make mockups with thin strips of wood or heavy-duty cardboard (the kind that is almost like a thin veneer). Secure the pieces with a glue gun and mark the orientation of where and how it fits. Using a mockup frame allows you to adjust your form to account for out of square, etc. and allows you to build a form that is a perfect fit.  Even though I consider myself an experienced woodworker, it took me several days to build the forms. One reason was I knew that the form would dictate the overall success of the project, so I really took my time. My forms were somewhat complicated too- I had to allow for cutouts, etc. Michael kind of glosses over the construction of the forms, but trust me, you will want to take your time with it. My bar tops had curved corners, so I used Michael‚Äôs other video that shows how to make flexible forms. I used the heavy-duty cardboard from the mockup frame covered in packing tape to protect them from moisture and it worked perfectly.  I tried Michael‚Äôs trick of using foam window cleaner during the caulking process. Maybe it was me, but it did not work that well. I ended up running a Sharpie along the caulk line, letting it dry for a day, and then taking a razor and scraping the edges off. The caulk comes off the melamine well and I got good results. It just took longer than Michael‚Äôs method. Another thing- make sure that you cover the screw heads with a small piece of duct tape. You will slop some of the mortar mix over the top of the forms and down the sides, so the time that it will take to cover the heads is drastically less than you will take trying to scrape mortar out of the screw heads. Trust me.  I bought the mortar mix, flow control, and set control for the pour. Even though I had three people assisting me, I was concerned that the mortar was going to set up too quickly. In retrospect, I do not think I needed the set control. It was a moderate day (72F) and setting up too quickly was not an issue. I highly recommend the flow control- it's amazing what it does to the mortar to make it pour easier. I put a tarp down in my garage and spread out the forms on sawhorses, buckets, and anything else that I could find to elevate the pieces. I also made sure they were level. I used the recommended water amount, plus a little over 3 ounces of colorant. Another tip- pour the mortar mix into the bucket with the water SLOWLY- anything past a healthy flow can result in water popping up and out of the bucket. With the colorant in the water, this might be more of an issue than if you were using just plain water. The pour went fairly quickly- I mixed and poured 9 bags of mortar mix in about two hours. Watch the torque on your drill while mixing. Even at low speed, there's a healthy amount of torque on the drill that can spin it around and whip into your hands. Be prepared. After the pour, I tapped the sides and bottoms of each form with a rubber mallet and sprayed the slabs with water often.  I waited a whole day before I broke up the forms. With the use of WD40, I was able to pull the forms apart easily. Overall, I was impressed with the results. With a little help this process is much easier because you can have one person mixing the mortar, one pouring, and another screeding and smoothing.  A couple last tips/observations- if your forms are even slightly out of level, it will create a slight bulge of mortar on the low side of the form. This is problematic when you take the forms off, because the bulge tends to break off, creating an unsightly edge that will have to be ground off to make it even with the rest of the edges. This happened to me on a couple of pieces and has created more work to look its best. Also be prepared for non-consistent color patterns. The larger bar tops have these weird colored concentric rings that are part of mortar. I can only assume that they were created from the multiple-bucket pours, even though the pours were less than 10 minutes apart.  Overall, I think I had good success for a first timer. The videos definitely helped (thanks Michael!) and I wouldn‚Äôt hesitate to try it again if I needed/wanted to.

How do you keep the grout mix from running when you poured the concrete
Well made and edited video. A few suggestions for those following these tips: - Make sure your mould is level in all directions before pouring and on strongly supported. - Add steel mesh or even glass fibres after you have the mix already poured on the mould surface. You may not need the added strength but it should not add much to the cost and you avoid the risk of the slab breaking while being moved. - Bring concrete/mortar mixture to the top of the form before screening it smooth. This way you have a smooth straight finish which will be easier to fit. - I would use less water as the finished product will be much stronger. Vibrate form for longer to reduce the number of air bubbles on the surface. - I made a sample mould and poured a 400mm square concrete slab so as to learn how this process works before pouring for a large Kitchen worktop.  Enjoy

Thanks for posting your method - super helpful! I ended up with some bubbles that i would like to fill. Do you recommend using the Mortar Mix to fill those?
We used this technique and had great success. No problems with sticking to the molds. Really no problems. Having the mortar thin resulted in almost no holes (total of 1 on an edge for an entire kitchen worth of countertops. I did the grout pattern on a coffee table and I will say that you need to use VERY LITTLE or you end up with holes. I mean very little in the depth. We added glitter to our counter tops and charcoal coloring. Turned out spectacularly. TIPS: 1. after it sets up a little go through and really clean the mold right up to the main pour. Some of ours were adhered to the mold and though we had no problems at all removing the mold, it did cause the edge to chip a little and wasn't smooth. 2. If you are pouring a large area have more than one person and more than one drill/paddle mixing bags bc you don't want it to get dry on you and it does quickly. 3. make sure it is liquidy enough. Only on 1 countertop did we pour it too dry and the surface wasn't quite as smooth in that area. (the others are SO SMOOTH) 4. mix up the colors. We poured 2 colors next to each other (2 grays) and it ended up looking really cool, but DO NOT POUR INTO A PREVIOUS COLOR. If you do you will get a circle where the new color poured through. That isn't pretty. (we did a practice piece and learned this.) 5. Make a small one first and practice. (we made a coffee table as a practice piece. It was fun and we learned a lot)

Tried this over the weekend. Mixed first 55lb bag with 5 quarts of water to start. I've mixed many buckets of mortar, so I'm not a rookie. First bucket set in 5 minutes, I was adding water and had to grab a nail bar to pry out my mixer. 2nd try. Started with 5 quarts water. Mixed the bag 1/4 of it at a time. Added flow Control at halfway point. Used over 7 quarts of water for the mix to seem right. Total mix time for 2nd bucket, maybe 8 minutes. I was worried that it was too thin. Nope, it literally set solid as I was pouring. Like a Wylie coyote cartoon. 3rd try... About to give up here. 5qts water to start slowly mix in bag of mortar. Add flow control. Bingo worked fine. Maybe a bit watery even.  Can't believe there is such a difference from bag to bag!

I decided to try this for four countertops in my house.  They turned out pretty good, but I have some tips as I ended up with some cracks.   1. Don't try anything fancy.  I tried molding a sink opening and some cutouts, but the mortar broke.  Which leads me to... 2. If you add reinforcement, i would try smaller steel than rebar.  I added rebar, but the mortar cracked around it. 3. I found 4 qts of water made a better mixture than 5. 4. If you add designs, careful on the amount of mortar, I ended up with some big voids. 5. Be careful on the type of silicone and make sure it gets a good layer of wd40.  The stuff I used bonded to the concrete in many places, and I spent a lot of time trying to get it off. 6. Let them dry longer than an hour, the ones I let dry for 24 hours worked better.  Make sure to keep them wet as long as possible.  The 1 hour strength is good, but why not let it get stronger? 7. Make sure to have a really good 1/2 inch drive drill with a paddle mixer, or a backup.  I burned through (literally burned through) a cheap drill and had to borrow one, so I lost a batch of concrete and a bucket.  I mixed 10 bags total. 8. Unscrew one side of the mold to make taking the mold off easier. Good job on the video, and keep 'em coming.  I am happy with the project, just had some tips for any first timers.

Nice Job Michael!  I followed your "recipe" to a tee... had a weird response to the flow control though; I was expecting pancake batter but got runny tomato soup.  I quickly mixed my second bag of Rapid Set in a second bucket, but didn't use flow control for that one.  Then started mixing "batches" into each other by pouring back and forth between the buckets until both buckets were close to the desired pancake batter.  Then I poured both buckets into the form and continued to follow the recipe.  It turned out great.  I was inside my workshop and it was 55 deg F outside when I mixed and poured.  Water was probably 55 deg too.  BTW - MAKE SURE YOUR FORM IS PERFECTLY LEVEL.  When you start vibrating it down with the orbital sander, the mix WILL level up.  I poured right to the top of the form and if the form is out of level by 1/8", you'll see it right away.  I used some shims to dial it in.  Made a concrete top for a new china cabinet for my wife for Mother's Day.  She loved it!

Just a couple of comments about "my first try" at making a concrete counter top.  I watched your video 3 times.  I took notes.  I thought I had this down, especially since I have done bathroom tile work and I have worked with epoxy grout.  No problem, I said.  I got this! My plan was for a barbecue counter top that measured 2 feet x 6 feet x 1.5 inches.  I did the math, 3 bags of Rapid Set Mortar. Made my form and caulked the corners.  Everything is going to plan. First the mixing: - I did exactly what you did for mixing my first bag and all of the water rose to the top leaving me with a large dry clump on the bottom of the bucket.  I had to dig into it to finally get it to mix with the water on top.  Added the Flow control powder and like a miracle I got the fluid mixture as shown in your video.  I poured it into my form and spread over the area. - Immediately started my second bucket with the water already pre-measured.  I added about half of the Mortar Mix and then added the rest and the Flow Control.  Maybe 15 minutes total mixing time.  By the time I got back to my mold the first mix was already setting.  And then the same thing happened with the third bucket. There is no way I could mix the mortar quick enough, working by myself. Disclaimer: I live in a very dry area of the country with low humidity and it was a fairly breezy day.  This probably accelerated the rapid drying time. Some observations:   - In your first video you show up with the second bucket of mortar mix very quickly.  and your fist batch is still fairly fluid.  Did you have some off-camera assistance? - For multi-bag pours I would highly recommend a second person to be doing the mixing so there is no delay in the batches. - Home Depot also carries a Rapid Set product called "Set Control".  It looks like this delays the drying time.  Have you ever used it? Conclusion:  I would add a few more cautions to your videos. First time?  Start with a smaller form. More than one bag mix?  Have second pair of hands so you can mix them at/near the same time. We learn more from our failures than our successes. Already looking forward to my next attempt.
Not trying to be rude at all.  Just honestly want to know the answer because I don't know anything about concrete.  If it is so strong why was it so easy to break off the edges to make the live edge and would this stand up to daily use as a kitchen countertop?  I really want to do this myself.  I just don't want to have to replace it in a year.  Any advice would be appreciated.

1st video???? Hard to believe, VERY well done! Excellent project, will be on my "to do list" for the summer! Thanks for posting.
I used this method over the weekend to cast a 2' x 8' x 2" outdoor bar top. In general, it worked very well. A couple of notes and other things I learned:    1. If you haven't done this before (I hadn't), and if it's possible with your time or budget or desired level of effort outlay, I would highly recommend a "test cast" of either a full or half bag of mortar in a small mold, before making your finished product. Its really good to get your technique down and practice! A small mold would be about 2'x2'x1.5" which would be a single 0.5 cu/ft bag.   2. I used the WD-40 as recommended, however I forgot to wipe it down before pouring. Turns out this is NOT an optional step. My slab has very evident graffiti-like markings where I sprayed the WD-40. Frankly, the melamine is so slick I'm not even sure this step is needed. Again, a test casting would have found this issue.    3. Make sure whatever structure you are casting on is ROCK SOLID and doesn't allow for any sagging in the mold as the concrete cures. The melamine is surprisingly resistant to deflection but remember that each bag of mix adds 55+ lbs of weight and that adds up quickly. I had a large corner crack off my casting and I attribute that to the mold sagging in the middle over the sawhorses I had it placed on as the concrete cured.
Question. I did this build with same products and measurements. Kept it wet for an hour, then let it dry before I flipped it. When flipping it over the middle fell out. I did put a 2" PVC coupler in middle for an umbrella to go into. Size is 3'6" x 6'6". 4 bags put me a quarter inch short from edge of mold. Something this big should I use some rebar? Also, should I mix each bag then dump them all or mix and pour, mix and pour.......

One thing you could do when making the live edge is to clamp a scrap board right near the edge, so you can't make a miss and clop off more than you intended. The board would take any hits not right on the edge... I doubt if you'd get too much of a line from this, the concrete would still fracture up under the clamped wood, but it would keep your strikes in a very narrow range... An awesome video. How anyone gave it a dislike I don't know. I just poured a 7 foot x 3 foot countertop for my outside bar. I included some simple grout designs, which taking the advice from another comment I sealed with hairspray. Although I've worked with concrete before, this was my first experience using rapid set. Lessons learned: Rapid set means just that! I didn't add the full 5 quarts of water, added 2.5 and started mixing. After a couple of minutes it started to go off, with the result that I ended up with a boat anchor. Never mind. Back to Home Depot for another bag. Only reason I wish I'd seen this before I made mine (and I only watched 2 videos previously) is because they had me use a cement with rocks in it which isn't a big problem but I would have preferred something more smooth.   One reason I am glad I watched the others first is because you didn't add any mesh. Unless your table is 4' or shorter, and it might be, you should supposedly be using mesh for strength.   Also, to anyone using a sealer, I used Quikcrete Cure and Seal and it's fine but if you do not put it on with a short nap roller meant for smooth surfaces it will leave brush marks. I put mine on with a foam brush at first, worked wet, and it still dried with a noticeable texture to the sheen. I've gone back over it 24 hours later with the roller and it's still showing through but will hopefully be better.

Great video.
Concrete strength ratings are for compression. The rebar used in concrete is for tensile strength. Adding rebar or wire mesh placed about 1/3 of the thickness of the pour from the bottom keeps the concrete from cracking when bending forces are applied. A tabletop is not likely to have bending loads.  That's the reason not to have to add it to this type of application.

I have done this and had to figure out how to get those heavy tops into tight places. Here is the fix. Put down plywood first! Place adhesive on plywood. Lift top onto plywood. Tilt up front edge and use golf balls to roll top into tight places. Remove golf balls with dowel. My tops were about 400 LBS. It worked perfectly. Good luck on your projects.

a little head up for bigger project. be ready to pour quickly as the first batch will start cooking the following one afterward. the continuous spray bottle is an absolute must to wet the slab (unles you're outside and have access to a hose with mist option). bigger slab also means a lot heavier! think about where you are pourring to avoid loosing level on grass or even avoiding breaking what ever you are pouring on. Also keep in mind to have some friends on hand to help you move the slab (one bag is 55 pound, an 8 foot by 2 foot slab will require about 6 so just do the math) dont hurt yourselve guys

Thank you, thank you, thank you! Just completed my first three of four cast pieces using your technique. Took five bags of the RapidSet Mortar across three forms.   Notes: -After mixing five bags, one immediately after another, the first form had already started to solidify and I did not get any bubbles vibed out. BUT the results are practically void free and look great. I attribute this to the Flow Control. I was averaging about three minutes a bucket, and I had all 3 forms filled in under fifteen minutes. The RapidSet lives up to its name and sets FAST! Next time I get the wife to vibe it with the sawzall as I pour. -Instead of a spray bottle, I would use a garden hose with a misting nozzle. Pumping water for an hour was a PITA. Maybe put this in the back yard and keep a sprinkler on it to wet the cement constantly after it starts to kick. -The black silicone was a little difficult to remove from places and stained the cement. I eventually used a nylon abrasive cup brush in a drill to polish the top and it blended in well. Almost thinking that white silicone might be more appropriate. It will look good. Sealed and ready for placement.  Used a silicone edge moulding. Worked well. Used a grinder with a flap pad to clean and soften edges.  I've got confidence that in an hour I'll build the final form, and another hour to cast it. Easier than ever. Thanks again! Dave Atwater Cedar Rapids, Iowa

Engineering problem: No matter how strong your concrete is it still only has great compression strength and does not have any tensile strength. So, when you put a bunch of heavy items on your counter time after time and year after year, your counter is going to experience compression stress. Since this doesn't happen in a vacuum, while the top is experiencing compression stress the bottom will be experiencing tensile stress. Maybe you'll be okay for a long time, maybe not. But adding metal, which provides the tensile strength shouldn't be hard even with your method. I am sure you could figure out how to float some wire in there. :-)

Just a small correction and to give back some knowledge to the community, the strength of concrete is typically provided as the compression strength (f'c) not the tensile strength (4:14ish). Concrete works really well as a member in compression but poorly as a tension member as it only has a fraction of the compressive strength for tension (roughly 10%!!). When you bend a piece of concrete (13:55) it cracks and breaks typically due to the tensile forces. One solution is to add a material to take on the tensile forces if they are high. Enter steel (rebar) or other hybrid materials. Steel is fantastic in tension but easily buckles under compression. It is like concrete and steel were made for each other! An easier solution for the DIYer is to make the table thicker but obviously that makes the table heavier. Typical concrete weight is 150 lb/ft3 (not so sure about mortar/quick cement). -Structural Engineer

Get out of Town. Great video.
Great video. I just tried out a similar project, a 60"x30"x1.25" slab. I'm a photographer and I'm always looking to make new surfaces to use for shooting on, so I thought a concrete counter/slab would be fun to experiment with. I used black tinting on mine to give it a deeper grey color. The one thing I did not do the same as you (but will next time) is use the flow control. The mortar bag says to use 3-3.75 qts water per bag for highway use (extra hi-strength) and UP TO 5 quarts for less critical stuff. I started with 4 quarts, but it was clear I'd need all 5 qts (as I see you did upon re-watching). It was the exact same consistency, THICK and smooth. It troweled awesome, but I had to work fast to get the other bags mixed and into the form before the prior one set too much. Since my mix was thicker ( no flow control!) it would NOT let any bubbles come to the surface, it just sat there like Jabba the Hutt.  Frankly I didn't try too hard to get air out. Anyway, I troweled the top (soon to be "underside") smooth, didn't even screed it, as it was setting up stiff. Then sprinkled on some light grey grout powder (thanks for the idea) like powdered sugar on a cookie, and troweled it into the surface to give it some tone variation.  I was quite surprised at how HOT it got! Not sure if you mentioned that in your video. It was like black asphalt on a sunny summer day.  I did use rebar in mine since I'll be lifting it and moving it around instead of permanently fixing it to a base, just wanted to be sure it doesn't split later.  When I popped it out of the form, there were a fair amount of tiny air bubble pockets present; it kind of looks like travertine marble, the unfilled side, except grey. BUT I actually like the texture. If it was a kitchen counter, it would be a FAIL, but for my purposes, I now have a 2 sided slab, that looks different each view.  I may use floor wax on it tomorrow after it dries out more to give it some stain resistance, or maybe a silicone sealer, not sure. I'll definitely be using this product more. Thanks again for turning me on to it!!
Dude... absolutely fantastic stuff. Can not wait to get to the Depot and give it a try. Thanks for your hard work!

I liked, saved and will be sharing your post. I also subscribed. Your video posts was clear, clean and to the point. (The best part is that I learned absolutely nothing about your personal life! LOL) But you missed a few biggies. Inverted drywall buckets (aka; those cute depot fashion bags) lack the required buoyancy to float on lawns. The evidence is that you could strike off one end of your frame and not the other. Leveling beforehand is not a guarantee of an end product with uniform thickness. Perfect for your in-law's den, but would not pass a woman's scruitany the first time dirty dishwasher ran down the front of her cabinets. But I think you saw it coming. Next time, maybe throw a couple of those pallet slats down first to keep your buckets from sinking? Or, ditch the buckets and put your mold on one of your better pallets that will remain leveled.    My next concern regards the rustic embellishments, but this may be another female thing. If your buddy has kids, he may not like digging macaroni and cheese out of the battlefield stencil. I'd make a suggestion on a fix but I'm not sure what would work to solve the problem without compromising the design. Can the cavities be filled with epoxy and sanded or would it yellow? Could the coloring be applied wet? Or, perhaps you could treat the powder areas as a sort of reverse stencil, blow out some of the remaining powder with a compressor or canned air, fill the gaps with the same or a secondary color mixed with tile cement (not grout). Maybe mix the tile cement with that sealer grout additive that is mold resistant.    But this is the game changer: I'm sure your gift will be well received. It looks great! But working with cement can expose the handler and any children or animals standing by to severe health risks, even when the work is done in a well ventilated area. Not only is cement a concentrated alkaloid, but CEMENT LOVES TO CLING TO ANYTHING MOIST. It especially likes the moist lining of our lungs. It has been known to coat the inside of both lungs with one breath. This might be the fastest way of making a permanent cast of you pulmonary cavity but it could make for a long clean-up time. LOL

Nice personable cool video man!!
First of all great video man! So I just recently built on outdoor bar top using all the steps in your video. Here are some things I learned after building mine (since i now know what to do i will rebuild this in the fall) My measurements were 96in long, 30in wide  and 2.25in deep. #1 do not attempt to mix this rapid set mortar mix in hot weather (above 85 degrees) because it will harden almost instantly. I went through 3 bags of mortar/flow control/ home depot buckets before I got so mad I almost gave up. Waited until later in the day (about 65 degrees) and then it mixed just like the video. And yes I used 55lb bags and 5 qts of water just like in the video. #2 get more bags of mortar mix and flow control than you think you need (you can always take them back) I used 4 bags for my measurements and it wasn't quite even with the sides. So I smoothed it out, let it sit and ran back to home depot for more mortar. It was already hardened when I returned so I just mixed the other bag and poured it on top to level it out all the way to the top. BIG MISTAKE! The second layer never bonded properly to the first layer and it just flakes off very easily (probably because it's so thin) #3 when you are making the live edge USE A BOARD UNDERNEATH WHERE YOU ARE HITTING! Some big pieces broke off and I had to glue them back with construction adhesive.  #4 the sanded tile grout works great for the stencil but it will leave the mortar porous in those places so definitely seal the counter top. (Which he has another great video on how to do!) Sorry for the super long comment but I wanted to share what I experienced so it might help others. All in all this video is awesome and yes the cast really does just come right off of the mortar if you do it just like the video shows (I was really worried about that lol)
Any idea if it‚Äôs okay to substitute something more food-safe than WD-40, like maybe olive oil?
Awesome project. One tip though. It may have been easier to flip the top over and set your wood base frame on it after adding your adhesive. Well done video but I have a lot of doubts on the top... there is zero reo in it and going for high strength concrete mixes just serves to make a more brittle top.. with such high strength and zero ability to flex, it'll take 1 firm knock and your top is done for. Also less of an issue as you can always do it, but no sealer means Karens red wine that inevitably ends up on it will leave a lasting reminder. an easy (not perfect but car better) option for your top emthod would have been to at least drop some SL52 or similar reo in during the pour.   In your instance these concerns aren't so problematic as your top is fully supported, but if someone tries this method on a suspended top they are doomed to fail.

Great video and interesting take on this by using mortar mix for a quick cure. I'm assuming you can add concrete dye to mortar mix just like with concrete if you want to darken it up. Only suggestion I'll add is don't glue your top onto your base. These things are HEAVY and it's a lot more convenient down the road if you can separate the top from the base. I got my top perfectly square on my base then glued 4 wood blocks to the underside of the concrete, pressed against the base supports at the corners. You can't see them unless you lay under the table and look up but I still stained them to match the base. Leave a small gap, 1/16th or less between the blocks and the base. For the top to move in any direction you have to lift it over these 4 key blocks and there's no way it's going to get lifted that much by accident with as much as it weighs. If you need to move it though I can easily lift the top straight off, set it aside, put the base where you want it and then set the top back on it. Far less likely to damage something moving it in two pieces like than than trying to move the heavy top with the base glued on permanently.

Nice video and good content, but I had to ride my volume control the whole time. I don't know why your volume is all over the place, but you should look into audio mastering techniques. I'd probably manually do some clip levelling and put a compressor on the output. You could use a ducker (compressor with a side chain) to lower the level of the music while talking. Just subtle, you don't want people to notice that it's happening. It would use the voice signal to automatically engage a compressor on the music channel. Do the levelling on both the music and narration channels separately first. Some clips might need a little EQ to match the others, but your EQ seems fine overall.

Generally good video. I will point out, though, that concrete generally cracks in tension, not compression. So, the fact that it develops a 6500 psi compressive strength does imply that no reinforcement is needed. It may be true that no reinforcement is needed. But, if so, it would be because the amount of tensile force acting on either the bottom or the top of the slab does not exceed the tensile strength of that surface, after the slab has been installed.

Michael got an email address. I want to share a couple of photos.   Thanks for the inspiration.   Poured my first one yesterday. This definately will not be my last. Made a few errors. OOPS. That is how I learn.  A few tips for people who are not Michael.  Buckets are fine if this is a one off thing. If you plan to do more. And yes plan to do more because once your neighbors and family see them. Well you know.  Make a table about 30 to 35 inches high. Yes you have to lift the bucket higher. But you wont have to continually stoop over.  I tried to put a design in mine. I failed miserably at this. Not sure what went wrong yet. So will try again.  I fixed it after the table dried. I took some of the charcoal grout I had left over. Mixed it and skimmed over the surface.  I did notice when pouring your first bucket try to get it to totally cover the surface of your pour. I could see the two different buckets in the final project. This will also help with curing evenly. If I was going to leave the counter raw I would not have wanted to see this.  For those whose troweling skills are not reasonable. IE not Michael or do not have experience at working masonry.  As you are troweling the bottom flat.   Keep your trowel clean. Remove any build up with a 6 in drywall knife. Clean with water occasionally.  The front end of the trowel is called the TOE. The back end the HEEL. Press down on the TOE and let the trowel marks form on the heel. Wipe off the trowel and then place the TOE about 2 inches past the trowel mark and repeat until you work your way off the surface and there are no more trowel marks.  You may notice ripples forming behind the trowel this is normal. If you want it slick keep troweling.  You have to let it start to harden so you quit moving anything but the surface material.  Get a small sprayer bottle and fill it with water. As the surface starts to dry to where you can trowel without dragging material.  Lightly mist the surface. Then trowel. It will help make it smooth.  The only way you are going to learn is if you try it.  Mine is going to be a table next to the smoker.

Great vid.  A couple of pointers for those who are not as used to working with fast setting mortar as Michael is.  Rapid Set Mortar sets (sets = maintains shape, but without a lot of strength) in 15 minutes at 70 deg F (20 C).  That means at 70 you've got about 10-12 minutes to work with it.  At 80 that becomes about 7 minutes and at 90 you're fighting to get it out of the bucket right after you mix it.  So mix and get it out of the bucket as fast as you can.  It's not like a regular mortar that gives you a lot of time to play.  M's method of putting in the Flow Control after mixing the mortar works well, but a way to gain a little bit of speed is to add the Flow Control to the water first and then add the Mortar.  That gives you quicker and easier mixing with less stress on your drill.

As a retired concrete finisher, who did a lot of work for people who had far more dollars than they did sense, this isn't bad, though for personal preference, you can't beat a good hand troweled surface and tooled edges, which makes for a more granite and/or marble clone type top..... Yes, every one I ever worked for told me I was way too fussy to do concrete work and that type of skill level is beyond most concrete finishers...... Never liked the plastisizers, or spastisizers as we used to call them. Turned the concrete into silly putty.... I'm guessing you spent at least 10 hours setting up the various shots and doing the video editing.  A couple constructive criticisms, cause I think you did a good job, but we can always get better. (1) The "grab the camera" transition got old after the second time.  You put so much work into pouring out the shot and the "snap fingers" cuts... the "grab camera cut" just bothers me 'cause it looks like amateur hour where someone grabs the camera to stop recording. (2) Put up a material list and/or budget on screen.  It makes it more user friendly.  I can't stand how cooking shows don't do this anymore.  The implication is that people aren't going to actually do the project, they are just watching for entertainment.  People who want to do it end up having to go back and watch over, or read the blog, etc. (3) Don't use the phrase "spice it up" in a construction video.  Ever. Martha Stewart can maybe get away with it, but I don't think you'd ever hear Norm Abrams say "spice it up". He'd say something like "The live edge will add a dimension to the surface by bringing out the coloration of the coarses and fines in our mortar mix. And you can even take it to the next level by adding crushed colored glass or colored pottery fragments, just make sure they are small enough ... blah blah blah."  Otherwise, first rate job. for tighter lines i would recommend making a small amount of concrete like less than a pint (no flow control), and just staining it with a concrete pigment and then put painters tape over your design and use a putty knife to spread the concrete over your design, then lift the design and clean it up where it needs it, then pour the rest of the concrete though this is more work the design would turn out very crisp. for viens i would recommend using some pigment to give one bucket of cement more color and then pouring one bucket into another ( you will need another pair of hands for this.)

#1 assemble the table upside down so you're not smearing construction adhesive everywhere, wait for it to cure before trying to flip it back unless you can get a helper.   #2 to help the new wood cuts to look similar to the rest (silver barnwoody type), you can age it by letting a bit of steel wool rust in 1/4 cup vinegar overnight, then dilute it 1:1 with water and put it on like a wood stain.  It won't be a perfect match but it'll be a lot less noticeable than fresh wood cuts. #3 the flow control mixing in shot was awesome, good job.

Congrats for your first video! Job well done. Have you thought of making a counter top table with concrete top?
This looks like a pretty simple method for making a countertop but if you're making one that has to fit against a wall, in a corner, etc...it's a whole different ballgame. I used to fab solid surface tops and you have to make pretty good stencils and take very precise measurements to make sure they fit right. Rarely will you have a perfectly straight wall. Of course your fitment against a wall, and whether you may see a gap against the wall or not,  will also depend on what you're using for a backsplash.  Something else...as far as adding a design or color is concerned, seeing as how it's still raw concrete when it comes out of the mold, you can use concrete stain right on the surface of the top. You can also use latex paint pigments as they will stain the concrete, as well. Doing tile jobs, I have used latex additives in my grout for years.  To color it, if you are looking for a specific color, take your sample to your paint store and let them computer match it.  Have them squirt the pigments into a can, coffee cup, or whatever...then carefully add it to your grout or concrete when you mix it. With grout I would suggest test mixing a very small batch first, keeping track of how much pigment you added to your mix, then let it kick off so you will what it looks like. Once you have your ratio right, mix your finish batch and grout away. This method should work just as well for painting a design concrete tops. Of course you would likely need to seal if afterwords.

If you plan on doing a lot of these or have a big single project around the house I would advise investing in a low rpm corded drill that's meant for mixing. Using a high prm drill will burn the motor up in no time. I operate and own my own tile showet company. I forgot my low rpm drill at home on a job 2 hrs away and burnt my dewalt drill after mixing about 15 buckets. Just a heads up for anyone wanting to do a lot of mixing. Thanks for introducing this product to me.  The fast set time of 2500 psi in one hour is a time saver. I would have never thought it was a CSA cement ! The light color is a bonus for design colors.   I'm surprised they call it Mortar Mix since it has so many other uses. You definitely wouldn't want to use a traditional mortar mix because of the lime content and that it can't be used in thick pours(at least according to Sakrete). A few Home Depots keep stock on Quikrete concrete countertop bag mixes the last time I checked for about the same cost per pound than the Mortar Mix but I think this might be a better product. I would have thrown in some 3/8" rebar for some more strength during handling or at least some strips of cement block or brick steel joint reinforcement  I'm not too keen on the WD40 as a mold release because of potential bonding issues with any stains or sealers. WD40 has a lot of VOCs that evaporate and don't leave much oil behind but I'm sure a wipe down with acetone would clean the concrete since you didn't use much.  I prefer a furniture type carnuba wax like Johnson's Paste Wax.

I'm going to use this method to make a 3 inch thick bench top to set my 12" craftsman lathe on, with some metal studs set into it to bolt the lathe down. using wood for a bench top for a lathe is not good because as the humidity and temperature changes the wood swells and changes and the lathe bed goes out of level. I wish to have a more stable base for my lathe and this will do the trick and also be economical.   I had a look at the specs for the Rapid set morter mix and you can use epoxy paint to seal it after a few days. I'll use oil resistant epoxy to seal it after it "cures". I'll embed 2 thick top plates with threaded holes to bolt the lather down and 4 metal plates with threaded holes for the legs to bolt on.   Should be easy and and most important ROCK SOLID and stable. My lathe will stop twisting and changing its level and my parts I make on it will be better. The concrete base should also help damp vibration. It is well known to use huge slabs of concrete for really large machine tool bases, now I'll have a mini version to keep my lathe ROCK stable. The PSI rating is the COMPRESSION LOAD! Steel reinforcement is for TENSILE STRENGTH to reduce the chance of it breaking while you're carrying it, turning it over, and bumping into things! It is also to keep the mass in one piece if it does crack while picking it up so it doesn't just fall on your feet. It may not be a problem for a short span like this, but you're begging for trouble with anything longer.

In the Granite Sheds in Vermont the technique they use for the natural stone edge is called Rock Pitching or a Rock Pitched Edge. The use a straight edge  and a chisel and hammer. The straight edge is to keep the edge at the surface straight. But you still get the pitched surface of the edge.

Cool Vid, can't wait for an excuse to make one. Just a quick note to those who will say why use the flow powder, just add water for a thinner mix.  Adding too much water to concrete will weaken the final product.  It is part of a chemical reaction that depends on the  ratios of the components.  Too much water will not just dry out later, it will make the finished concrete weaker.

Please... A day to design the form,  two days minimum to purchase the materials  and build the form  (assuming no sink opening or unusual shape needed). One half day to mix and pour. One half day to release concrete from form.  Half day to set countertop in place. 4 1/2 days minimum - more if you are coloring, sealing or otherwise refinishing .  Then cleanup however long that takes.  Please. Tricksie camera work does not make the process shorter or less labor intensive.

Great video, but if you are going to give it a "RockFace" use a chalk line and a Brickset  aka chisel for stone, not a Brick Hammer. you need any hammer and a Brickset chisel they are wide and a 20 degree angle will make a nice sharp edge with a great rockface look, the edge will be straight and crisp.

Avoid skin contact with CSA mortars and cements, especially with your hands. The aluminum in the cement is very hard on the nerves in your hands. Clean-up water is often the place where the aluminum gets the most skin contact. I almost had to retire when my hands became so bad until I changed to a Portland based cement mortars and grouts.
